<p><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy&#39;s signature"><img alt="John F. Kennedy&#39;s signature" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/John_F_Kennedy_Signature_2.svg/150px-John_F_Kennedy_Signature_2.svg.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="35" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/John_F_Kennedy_Signature_2.svg/225px-John_F_Kennedy_Signature_2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/John_F_Kennedy_Signature_2.svg/300px-John_F_Kennedy_Signature_2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="391" data-file-height="91" /></a><br />
<img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg/70px-Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg.png" decoding="async" width="70" height="70" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg/105px-Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg/140px-Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2424" data-file-height="2425" />
</p>
<a href="/wiki/List_of_personal_coats_of_arms_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of personal coats of arms of Presidents of the United States"><img alt="Coat of Arms of John F. Kennedy.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Coat_of_Arms_of_John_F._Kennedy.svg/40px-Coat_of_Arms_of_John_F._Kennedy.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="60" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Coat_of_Arms_of_John_F._Kennedy.svg/60px-Coat_of_Arms_of_John_F._Kennedy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Coat_of_Arms_of_John_F._Kennedy.svg/80px-Coat_of_Arms_of_John_F._Kennedy.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="395" data-file-height="588" /></a></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;text-align:center;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:JFK_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Template:JFK series"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:JFK_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Template talk:JFK series"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:JFK_series&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>foreign policy of the John F. Kennedy administration</b> was the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Foreign policy of the United States">foreign policy of the United States</a> from 1961 to 1963 while <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> was president. Interactions with foreign nations during this period included diplomatic and military initiatives in Western Europe, <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, and Latin America, all conducted amid considerable <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> tensions with the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe. Kennedy deployed a new generation of foreign policy experts, dubbed "the best and the brightest".<sup id="cite_ref-Hastedt_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastedt-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  In <a href="/wiki/Inaugural_address_of_John_F._Kennedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Inaugural address of John F. Kennedy">his inaugural address</a> Kennedy encapsulated his Cold War stance: "Let us never negotiate out of fear. But let us never fear to negotiate".<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy's strategy of <a href="/wiki/Flexible_response" title="Flexible response">flexible response</a>, managed by Defense Secretary <a href="/wiki/Robert_McNamara" title="Robert McNamara">Robert McNamara</a>, was aimed to reduce the possibility of war by miscalculation. His administration resulted in the peaceful resolution of the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a> and refrained from further escalation of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Crisis_of_1961" title="Berlin Crisis of 1961">Berlin Crisis of 1961</a>. However, Kennedy policies also led to implementing the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_invasion" class="mw-redirect" title="Bay of Pigs invasion">Bay of Pigs invasion</a> and escalation of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy was committed to the rapid economic development of the impoverished newly organized nations in the Third World.  He used <a href="/wiki/Modernization_theory" title="Modernization theory">modernization theory</a> as the model to follow, and created the  <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Progress" title="Alliance for Progress">Alliance for Progress</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Corps" title="Peace Corps">Peace Corps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_for_Peace" title="Food for Peace">Food for Peace</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Agency_for_International_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Agency for International Development">Agency for International Development</a> (AID). After the near escape from disaster in the Cuban Missile Crisis, he promoted disarmament and disengagement programs with Moscow, and created the <a href="/wiki/Arms_Control_and_Disarmament_Agency" title="Arms Control and Disarmament Agency">Arms Control and Disarmament Agency</a>. In October, 1963, he signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty">Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty</a>, which was accepted by Moscow and London.
</p>

</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_team">Leadership team</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Leadership team">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From election day until late December, Kennedy, aided especially by his brother <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a>, selected his foreign policy leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>  He kept a few prominent holdovers, including <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a> at the FBI and <a href="/wiki/Allen_Dulles" title="Allen Dulles">Allen Dulles</a> as <a href="/wiki/Director_of_Central_Intelligence" title="Director of Central Intelligence">Director of Central Intelligence</a>. <a href="/wiki/C._Douglas_Dillon" title="C. Douglas Dillon">C. Douglas Dillon</a>, a Republican who had served as Eisenhower's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Under_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Under Secretary of State">Undersecretary of State</a> was named Secretary of the Treasury.  <a href="/wiki/Robert_McNamara" title="Robert McNamara">Robert McNamara</a>, who was well known as one of <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Whiz_Kids_(Ford)" title="Whiz Kids (Ford)">Whiz Kids</a>", was appointed Secretary of Defense. Rejecting liberal pressure to choose <a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson</a> as Secretary of State, and ignoring the powerful senator from Arkansas <a href="/wiki/J._William_Fulbright" title="J. William Fulbright">J. William Fulbright</a>, the president instead turned to <a href="/wiki/Dean_Rusk" title="Dean Rusk">Dean Rusk</a>, a restrained former Truman official.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParmet198366-68_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParmet198366-68-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Stevenson accepted the mostly honorific appointment as the ambassador to the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio200620–21_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio200620–21-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Robert Kennedy was selected as Attorney General, and the younger Kennedy was often referred to as the "assistant president" in reference to his wide range of influence.<sup id="cite_ref-asstpresident_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asstpresident-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_John_F._Kennedy_with_His_White_House_Staff.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/President_John_F._Kennedy_with_His_White_House_Staff.jpg/210px-President_John_F._Kennedy_with_His_White_House_Staff.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/President_John_F._Kennedy_with_His_White_House_Staff.jpg/315px-President_John_F._Kennedy_with_His_White_House_Staff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/President_John_F._Kennedy_with_His_White_House_Staff.jpg/420px-President_John_F._Kennedy_with_His_White_House_Staff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6017" data-file-height="4668" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_John_F._Kennedy_with_His_White_House_Staff.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President John F. Kennedy (seated) with members of his White House staff</div></div></div>
<p>Kennedy generally assigned the State Department to handle routine issues while major foreign policy decisions were handled in the White House.  The President's own reputation was built largely on his knowledge of world affairs, going back to his senior thesis  at Harvard on British foreign policy in the 1930s. Kennedy found it very difficult to get domestic legislation through Congress, but discovered that he could make significant decisions on foreign policy without consulting Congress. He set up the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Corps" title="Peace Corps">Peace Corps</a> by Executive Order, and put his brother-in-law in charge. The national security council staff, which did not need Senate approval, became a little State Department, and was headed by  <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Advisor_(United_States)" title="National Security Advisor (United States)">National Security Advisor</a> <a href="/wiki/McGeorge_Bundy" title="McGeorge Bundy">McGeorge Bundy</a>, a Harvard professor.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>  Other key White House aides included speechwriter <a href="/wiki/Ted_Sorensen" title="Ted Sorensen">Ted Sorensen</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrinkley201255_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrinkley201255-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and advisers  <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.</a>, press secretary <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Salinger" title="Pierre Salinger">Pierre Salinger</a>, military expert General <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_D._Taylor" title="Maxwell D. Taylor">Maxwell D. Taylor</a>, and party leader <a href="/wiki/W._Averell_Harriman" title="W. Averell Harriman">W. Averell Harriman</a>.  Vice President Johnson had a minimal role in foreign policy; instead he was sent abroad on many ceremonial visits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio200641–43_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio200641–43-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CIA._intelligence_and_espionage">CIA. intelligence and espionage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: CIA. intelligence and espionage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="History of the Central Intelligence Agency">History of the Central Intelligence Agency</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_espionage" title="History of espionage">History of espionage</a></div>
<p>The credibility of the CIA was wounded at the Bay of Pigs.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> As a result director Allen Dulles was replaced in September 1961 by <a href="/wiki/John_A._McCone" title="John A. McCone">John A. McCone</a>, another  conservative Republican, after a brief battle in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Communist_states">Communist states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Communist states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The communist world under Soviet leadership split up in the Kennedy era, with the Soviet Union and China increasingly at swords point. The American strategy was to strongly oppose China, fearing that it had the greater potential to win support in the Third World. Kennedy saw an opportunity to deal with Moscow on friendlier terms.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Cold_War_and_flexible_response">The Cold War and flexible response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The Cold War and flexible response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flexible_response" title="Flexible response">Flexible response</a></div>
<p>Kennedy's foreign policy was dominated by American confrontations with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, manifested by proxy contests in the global state of tension known as the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. Like his predecessors, Kennedy adopted the policy of <a href="/wiki/Containment" title="Containment">containment</a>, which sought to stop the spread of Communism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring2008704–705_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring2008704–705-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> President Eisenhower's <a href="/wiki/New_Look_(policy)" title="New Look (policy)">New Look</a> policy had emphasized the use of nuclear weapons to <a href="/wiki/Deterrence_theory" title="Deterrence theory">deter</a> the threat of Soviet aggression. Fearful of the possibility of a global nuclear war, Kennedy implemented a new strategy known as <a href="/wiki/Flexible_response" title="Flexible response">flexible response</a>. This strategy relied on conventional arms to achieve limited goals. As part of this policy, Kennedy expanded the <a href="/wiki/United_States_special_operations_forces" title="United States special operations forces">United States special operations forces</a>, elite military units that could fight unconventionally in various conflicts. Kennedy hoped that the flexible response strategy would allow the U.S. to counter Soviet influence without resorting to war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrinkley201276–77_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrinkley201276–77-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, he ordered a massive build-up of the nuclear arsenal to establish superiority over the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring2008704–705_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring2008704–705-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In pursuing this military build-up, Kennedy shifted away from Eisenhower's deep concern for budget deficits caused by military spending.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996489–490_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996489–490-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In his 1960 presidential race, Kennedy  strongly criticized Eisenhower’s inadequate spending on defense. In his inaugural address he promised “to bear any burden” in the defense of liberty, and he repeatedly asked for increases in military spending and authorization of new weapon systems. From 1961 to 1964 the number of nuclear weapons increased by 50 percent, as did the number of B-52 bombers to deliver them. The new ICBM force grew from 63 intercontinental ballistic missiles to 424. He authorized 23 new Polaris submarines, each of which carried 16 nuclear missiles. He called on cities to prepare fallout shelters for nuclear war. In contrast to Eisenhower’s warning about the perils of the <a href="/wiki/Military%E2%80%93industrial_complex" title="Military–industrial complex">military–industrial complex</a>, Kennedy focused on rearmament.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He gave the Pentagon a global reach, with 275 major bases in 31 countries, with 1.2 million personnel stationed there. Kennedy used the military as a political instrument more often than any other postwar president, with 13 episodes a year compared to four a year under Truman; seven per year for Eisenhower; nine per year for Johnson; and five per year for Nixon and Ford.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_Union">Soviet Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Soviet Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK_Khrushchev_Handshake_1961.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/JFK_Khrushchev_Handshake_1961.jpg/250px-JFK_Khrushchev_Handshake_1961.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/JFK_Khrushchev_Handshake_1961.jpg/375px-JFK_Khrushchev_Handshake_1961.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/JFK_Khrushchev_Handshake_1961.jpg/500px-JFK_Khrushchev_Handshake_1961.jpg 2x" data-file-width="586" data-file-height="383" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK_Khrushchev_Handshake_1961.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy shaking hands with <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>, 1961.</div></div></div> 
<p>On November 29, 1961, American officials declared that the <a href="/wiki/Telegraph_Agency_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph Agency of the Soviet Union">Telegraph Agency of the Soviet Union</a> (TASS) allegedly distributed a distorted, editorialized version of the Kennedy interview, given to <i><a href="/wiki/Izvestiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Izvestiya">Izvestiya</a></i> employee Alexei Adzhubey. According to U.S. officials, the omissions included Kennedy's charges that the Soviets had violated the <a href="/wiki/Yalta_Conference" title="Yalta Conference">Yalta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Potsdam agreement">Potsdam agreements</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Moratorium_(law)" title="Moratorium (law)">moratorium</a> on <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_tests" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear tests">nuclear tests</a> and his claim that the issue of divided Berlin largely stems from the Soviet refusal to agree to German reunification.<sup id="cite_ref-Altus_Times_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altus_Times-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Adzhubey promised to publish the full text in <i>Izvestiya</i> and Kennedy publicly expressed his appreciation for that.<sup id="cite_ref-Altus_Times_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altus_Times-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 1961, Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> declared his support for <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_national_liberation" title="Wars of national liberation">wars of national liberation</a>. Kennedy interpreted this step as a direct threat to the "free world".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> On February 15, 1961, the President asked Soviets to avoid interfering with <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> pacification of the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">Congo Crisis</a>. Khrushchev proposed to amend the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Charter" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Charter">United Nations Charter</a> by replacing the position of <a href="/wiki/UN_Secretary-General" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Secretary-General">Secretary-General</a> with a three-person executive called the Troyka (Russian: "group of three"). On September 25, 1961, Kennedy addressed the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a>, revealing his commitment to veto the Troyka plan. On February 27 of that year, in his letter to Khrushchev, the President offered an early summit meeting. Khrushchev agreed to meet in the <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austrian</a> capital <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>. The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Vienna_summit" title="Vienna summit">Vienna summit</a> was tainted by the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion" title="Bay of Pigs Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</a>. Khrushchev, however, tended to attribute the responsibility for the invasion not to Kennedy, but to his subordinates.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During his meeting with Khrushchev, Kennedy's main goal was to suggest a retraction from the Cold War. Nonetheless, he did not believe that it would be feasible to change something either in divided Europe or in the Far East. Subsequently, he spoke with very general wording. However, Kennedy did take the novel step of emphasizing the importance of Allied access to West Berlin. Previous administrations had simply referred to "Berlin."  The evidence suggests that Kennedy essentially accepted the permanent division of Berlin into East and West and implied that an East Berlin border closure would not bring a US response as long as West Berlin was left alone. Since he was already thinking about putting up a wall in Berlin, Khrushchev was encouraged to continue down this path.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/U.S._State_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. State Department">U.S. State Department</a> prepared several papers for Kennedy on how to approach Khrushchev. One of them, titled "Scope Paper", indicated that Khrushchev would "undoubtedly press hard his position on Berlin and a peace treaty with <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In spring 1963, Kennedy started to seek a further conciliation with the Soviet Union. In the summer of that year, he sought to wind down the confrontational mentality that dominated <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union_%E2%80%93_United_States_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Soviet Union – United States relations">American–Soviet relations</a> and to replace standard anticommunist rhetoric with a conciliatory one.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Test_Ban_Treaty">Test Ban Treaty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Test Ban Treaty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty">Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty</a></div>
<p>Abstract: On 10 June 1963 Kennedy gave a speech that facilitated a major agreement with Moscow Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty. It helped  avoid a nuclear holocaust, since the nuclear confrontation was not then a stable balance of terror, but rather a highly unstable situation that was prone to accidents, misjudgements and escalating disaster. Presidential leadership played a decisive role. Following the Cuban Missile Crisis, Kennedy saw that only he could find the terms that would be accepted by Khrushchev nuclear war. The result was peace diplomacy that led to his collaboration with Khrushchev that succeeded in pulling the superpowers back from the brink. Khrushchev called it, "the best speech by any president since Roosevelt."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before the Cuban missile crisis, policymakers in Washington were uncertain whether or not China would break with the Soviet Union on the basis of ideology, national ambitions, and readiness for a role in guiding communist activities in many countries. New inside came with the Sino-Indian border war in November 1962 and Beijing's response to the Cuban Missile Crisis. Kennedy administration officials concluded that China was more militant and more dangerous than the Soviet Union, making better relations with Moscow desirable, with both nations trying to contain Chinese ambitions.  Diplomatic recognition of China remained out of the question, as a crucial veto power on the UN Security Council was held by America's ally on Taiwan.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Tensions escalated between the Moscow and Beijing, as Chinese leader <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> castigated Khrushchev's "capitulation" in the Cuban crisis. With a partial thaw in relations between the United States and the Soviet Union, China emerged as the biggest Cold War enemy in Kennedy's rhetoric.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring2008725–726_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring2008725–726-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  To rally support at home for his "<a href="/wiki/Great_Leap_Forward" title="Great Leap Forward">Great Leap Forward</a>", Mao deliberately made the United States a highly visible enemy, and focused even more hostility against India, to the point of <a href="/wiki/Sino-Indian_War" title="Sino-Indian War">low-level 33-day war along their long border in late 1962</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The United States supported India, ignoring India's long-standing commitment to Moscow.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> India realized it needed American financial help and munitions so Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a> in August 1963, wrote Kennedy explaining the challenges India faced from China and Pakistan. Nehru indicated his agreement with the American position when he warned that the Chinese were:
</p>
<dl><dd>making a bid for leadership not only in Asia but of the Communist world and this too only as a first step in their bid for world leadership....The Chinese want people in Afro-Asian and Latin American countries to adopt militant, aggressive and revolutionary attitudes and are against democratic evolutionary practices and stable regimes.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuban_Missile_Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cuban Missile Crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_-_signing_Cuba_Quarantine_Proclamation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/President_Kennedy_-_signing_Cuba_Quarantine_Proclamation.jpg/170px-President_Kennedy_-_signing_Cuba_Quarantine_Proclamation.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/President_Kennedy_-_signing_Cuba_Quarantine_Proclamation.jpg/255px-President_Kennedy_-_signing_Cuba_Quarantine_Proclamation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/President_Kennedy_-_signing_Cuba_Quarantine_Proclamation.jpg/340px-President_Kennedy_-_signing_Cuba_Quarantine_Proclamation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="666" data-file-height="842" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_-_signing_Cuba_Quarantine_Proclamation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy, signing the authorization of the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis#Quarantine" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">naval quarantine of Cuba</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>After the ill-fated Bay of Pigs Invasion, in late July 1962, the Soviet Union began sending its weaponry and military personnel to Cuba, citing the intents to protect Cuba from further invasions. The Soviet Union planned to allocate in Cuba 49 <a href="/wiki/Medium-range_ballistic_missile" title="Medium-range ballistic missile">medium-range ballistic missiles</a>, 32 <a href="/wiki/Intermediate-range_ballistic_missile" title="Intermediate-range ballistic missile">intermediate-range ballistic missiles</a>, 49 light <a href="/wiki/Ilyushin_Il-28" title="Ilyushin Il-28">Il-28</a> bombers and about 100 <a href="/wiki/Tactical_nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Tactical nuclear weapons">tactical nuclear weapons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After their discovery Kennedy secretly met with the <a href="/wiki/EXCOMM" title="EXCOMM">EXCOMM</a>. He postponed a military solution of the crisis strenuously advocated by the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff">Joint Chiefs of Staff</a>, and decided to impose a naval <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantine</a> on Cuba. On October 22, 1962 Kennedy informed the nation of the crisis, announcing the quarantine and demanding the removal of Soviet missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenney,_Charles_2000_pp._184-186_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenney,_Charles_2000_pp._184-186-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy managed to preserve restraint when a Soviet missile unauthorizedly downed a US <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">Lockheed U-2</a> reconnaissance aircraft over Cuba, killing the pilot <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Anderson" title="Rudolf Anderson">Rudolf Anderson</a>.  On October 27, in a letter to Nikita Khrushchev, Kennedy offered a noninvasion pledge for the removal of missiles from Cuba. The next day Kennedy and Khrushchev struck a deal: the Soviet Union agreed to remove the missiles in exchange for the United States' noninvasion pledge and the dismantlement of US <a href="/wiki/PGM-19_Jupiter" title="PGM-19 Jupiter">PGM-19 Jupiter</a> missiles based in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>. By that time, the fifteen Jupiter missiles were considered obsolete and had been supplanted by missile-equipped US Navy Polaris subs.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenney,_Charles_2000_pp._184-186_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenney,_Charles_2000_pp._184-186-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> They were removed the next year.
</p><p>During the crisis Kennedy showed his leadership talents, decision-making abilities and crisis management skills. By early November 1962 Kennedy's handling of the Cuban Missile Crisis was considered by most Americans as a diplomatic success in foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multipolarity_In_Europe">Multipolarity In Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Multipolarity In Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The United States and the Soviet Union had retained firm leadership of their respective coalitions throughout the 1950s, but both blocs began to fracture during Kennedy's term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring2008724–726_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring2008724–726-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> vetoed Britain's application to join the Common Market (European Economic Community) in January 1963 after appearing receptive to the idea just months earlier. De Gaulle pointed to the risk of a loss of cohesion in Common Market and the need to Maintain independence from the United States. He distrusted British intentions in Europe. His chief reason was Britain's deal with the U.S. through NATO involving Polaris nuclear missile technology. De Gaulle wanted a strong Europe free of any dependence on the United States, while Harold Macmillan and other British leaders considered their country's "special relationship" with the United States more important to its future.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In 1963, France and West Germany signed the <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lys%C3%A9e_Treaty" title="Élysée Treaty">Élysée Treaty</a>, marking even closer relations between the two countries. De Gaulle also rejected Kennedy's proposed <a href="/wiki/Multilateral_Force" title="Multilateral Force">Multilateral Force</a> in favor of an <a href="/wiki/France_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="France and weapons of mass destruction">independent nuclear weapons program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring2008724–726_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring2008724–726-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 1960 the United Kingdom had ceased their work on a national missile system and Eisenhower offered to make the American <a href="/wiki/GAM-87_Skybolt" title="GAM-87 Skybolt">GAM-87 Skybolt</a> available to the British as soon as it was improved. The United Kingdom accepted the offer as the GAM-87 Skybolt would have ensured it a nuclear deterrent through most of the 1960s. By mid-1962, however, Robert McNamara had deemed the Skybolt project "excessively expensive... with serious technical flaws"<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  and decided to cancel it.
</p><p>Because of informational mishaps Kennedy was not informed that McNamara's decision would have serious political consequences for <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a>'s government.<sup id="cite_ref-Siracusa_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siracusa-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> At a meeting with Macmillan, Kennedy attempted to save the situation and offered the United Kingdom the <a href="/wiki/UGM-27_Polaris" title="UGM-27 Polaris">UGM-27 Polaris</a> in lieu of Skybolt.<sup id="cite_ref-Siracusa_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siracusa-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The related agreement dissatisfied French President <a href="/wiki/Charles_De_Gaulle" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles De Gaulle">Charles De Gaulle</a>, who resented American preference toward Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Siracusa_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siracusa-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dinner_in_Honor_of_Andre_Malraux.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Dinner_in_Honor_of_Andre_Malraux.jpg/150px-Dinner_in_Honor_of_Andre_Malraux.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Dinner_in_Honor_of_Andre_Malraux.jpg/225px-Dinner_in_Honor_of_Andre_Malraux.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Dinner_in_Honor_of_Andre_Malraux.jpg/300px-Dinner_in_Honor_of_Andre_Malraux.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="766" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dinner_in_Honor_of_Andre_Malraux.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy at a White House dinner in honor of the French Minister of Cultural Affairs <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Malraux" title="André Malraux">André Malraux</a>, 1962.</div></div></div>
<p>France was the second  country Kennedy visited as President. He arrived to <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> with his wife <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacqueline Kennedy">Jacqueline Kennedy</a> on May 31, 1961. Charles De Gaulle, known for his preference to speak French to foreign guests, greeted Kennedy in English.<sup id="cite_ref-McElrath1_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McElrath1-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Jacqueline, who in turn spoke fluent French, intrigued the French press, which called her the "queen".<sup id="cite_ref-McElrath1_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McElrath1-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Force_de_Frappe" class="mw-redirect" title="Force de Frappe">French nuclear program</a> was pivotal in De Gaulle's aim of restoring France's international reputation. Kennedy administration had a firm commitment to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_nonproliferation" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear nonproliferation">nuclear nonproliferation</a>. In a letter to Harold Macmillan Kennedy wrote: "After careful review of the problem, I have to come to the conclusion that it would be undesirable to assist France's efforts to create a nuclear weapons capability".<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was particularly dissatisfied with De Gaulle's intentions to assist <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> in developing nuclear weapons.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_and_West_Germany">East and West Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: East and West Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Crisis_of_1961" title="Berlin Crisis of 1961">Berlin Crisis of 1961</a></div>
<p>Kennedy called <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> "the great testing place of Western courage and will". On August 13, 1961 the <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germans</a>, backed by <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, suddenly erected a temporary barbed wire barricade and then a concrete barrier, dividing Berlin. Kennedy noted that "it seems particularly stupid to risk killing millions of Americans... because Germans want Germany to be reunified".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F015779-0009,_Bonn,_Staatsbesuch_Pr%C3%A4sident_der_USA,_Kennedy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F015779-0009%2C_Bonn%2C_Staatsbesuch_Pr%C3%A4sident_der_USA%2C_Kennedy.jpg/250px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F015779-0009%2C_Bonn%2C_Staatsbesuch_Pr%C3%A4sident_der_USA%2C_Kennedy.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F015779-0009%2C_Bonn%2C_Staatsbesuch_Pr%C3%A4sident_der_USA%2C_Kennedy.jpg/375px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F015779-0009%2C_Bonn%2C_Staatsbesuch_Pr%C3%A4sident_der_USA%2C_Kennedy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F015779-0009%2C_Bonn%2C_Staatsbesuch_Pr%C3%A4sident_der_USA%2C_Kennedy.jpg/500px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F015779-0009%2C_Bonn%2C_Staatsbesuch_Pr%C3%A4sident_der_USA%2C_Kennedy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="604" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F015779-0009,_Bonn,_Staatsbesuch_Pr%C3%A4sident_der_USA,_Kennedy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy with the Chancellor of West Germany <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Adenauer" title="Konrad Adenauer">Konrad Adenauer</a> (center) in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, 1963.</div></div></div>
<p>Two months later, a US-Soviet war nearly occurred as US and Soviet tanks faced off across Checkpoint Charlie. The crisis was defused largely through a backchannel communication the Kennedy administration had set up with Soviet spy <a href="/wiki/Georgi_Bolshakov" title="Georgi Bolshakov">Georgi Bolshakov</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of the Berlin crisis Kennedy's government faced a dramatic increase in the <a href="/wiki/Defense_budget" class="mw-redirect" title="Defense budget">defense budget</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pagedas_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pagedas-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The negative <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_payments" title="Balance of payments">balance of payments</a> with the European allies had aggravated American fiscal problems. At the end of 1961 Robert McNamara concluded an arrangement with <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> whereby the latter was to annually purchase some American military hardware. However this only partially alleviated the payments issue.<sup id="cite_ref-Pagedas_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pagedas-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 26, 1963 Kennedy arrived in <a href="/wiki/West_Berlin" title="West Berlin">West Berlin</a> and visited <a href="/wiki/Checkpoint_Charlie" title="Checkpoint Charlie">Checkpoint Charlie</a>. On that day he delivered his famous "<a href="/wiki/Ich_bin_ein_Berliner" title="Ich bin ein Berliner">Ich bin ein Berliner</a>" speech in front of 150,000 West Germans. In remarks to his aides on <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a> Kennedy noted that the wall "is a hell of a lot better than a war".<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy ordered 500 military men to travel on trucks through East Germany to West Berlin to insure that the West preserved the land-link to the city. In late October 1961, a dispute over the right of one U.S. diplomat to cross the East Berlin flared into conflict. Soviet and American tanks faced one another at Checkpoint Charlie, but Kennedy through an intermediary offered Khrushchev a conciliatory formula and both superpowers withdrew their tanks.<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia_and_Middle_East">Asia and Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Asia and Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Kennedy's Asian initiatives particularly targeted <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> because it followed a noncommunist model of economic development and was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Nonaligned_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonaligned Movement">Nonaligned Movement</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel_and_Arab_states">Israel and Arab states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Israel and Arab states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Kennedy firmly believed in the U.S. commitment to <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israeli</a> security, but his Middle Eastern policy saw ambitious <a href="/wiki/Pan-Arabism" title="Pan-Arabism">Pan-Arabic</a> initiatives of <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>. In 1960, Kennedy stated: "Israel will endure and flourish. It is the child of hope and the home of the brave. It can neither be broken by adversity nor demoralized by success. It carries the shield of democracy and it honors the sword of freedom".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Subsequently, as president, Kennedy initiated the creation of security ties with Israel, and he is credited as the founder of the US-Israeli military alliance (which would be continued under subsequent presidents). Kennedy ended the arms embargo that the Eisenhower and Truman administrations had enforced on Israel. Describing the protection of Israel as a moral and national commitment, he was the first to introduce the concept of a 'special relationship' (as he described it to <a href="/wiki/Golda_Meir" title="Golda Meir">Golda Meir</a>) between the US and Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy extended the first informal security guarantees to Israel in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-Walt,_Stephen_M._1987_pp._95-96_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walt,_Stephen_M._1987_pp._95-96-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in 1963, Kennedy allowed the sale to Israel of advanced US weaponry (the <a href="/wiki/MIM-23_Hawk" title="MIM-23 Hawk">MIM-23 Hawk</a>), as well as to provide diplomatic support for Israeli policies which were opposed by Arab neighbours, such as its water project on the Jordan River.<sup id="cite_ref-Walt,_Stephen_M._1987_pp._95-96_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walt,_Stephen_M._1987_pp._95-96-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the summer of 1960 the U.S. embassy in <a href="/wiki/Tel_Aviv" title="Tel Aviv">Tel Aviv</a> learned that Israel was assisted by France in the construction of what U.S. intelligence called "a significant atomic installation" in <a href="/wiki/Dimona" title="Dimona">Dimona</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/David_Ben-Gurion" title="David Ben-Gurion">David Ben-Gurion</a> had publicly assured the United States that Israel did not plan to develop nuclear weapons, Kennedy tried unsuccessfully to persuade Israel to permit some qualified expert (either American or from some other friendly nation) to visit Dimona. According to <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Hersh" title="Seymour Hersh">Seymour Hersh</a>, the inspections were conducted in such a way that it "guaranteed that the whole procedure would be little more than a whitewash, as the president and his senior advisors had to understand: the American inspection team would have to schedule its visits well in advance, and with the full acquiescence of Israel."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marc_Trachtenberg" title="Marc Trachtenberg">Marc Trachtenberg</a> argued: "Although well aware of what the Israelis were doing, Kennedy chose to take this as satisfactory evidence of Israeli compliance with America's non-proliferation policy."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The American who led the inspection team stated that the essential goal of the inspections was to find "ways to not reach the point of taking action against Israel's nuclear weapons program."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1962 the United States sent to Israel the <a href="/wiki/MIM-23_Hawk" title="MIM-23 Hawk">MIM-23 Hawk</a> missiles. Nonetheless Kennedy wished to work more closely with the modernizing forces of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>. In June 1962 Nasser wrote Kennedy a letter, noting that though Egypt and the United States had differences, they could still cooperate.
</p><p>Following the outburst of the <a href="/wiki/North_Yemen_Civil_War" title="North Yemen Civil War">North Yemen Civil War</a> Kennedy, fearing that it would lead to a larger conflict between Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> (which might involve the United States as Saudi ally), decided to recognize the revolutionary regime. Kennedy hoped that it could stabilize the situation in Yemen. Kennedy still tried to persuade Nasser to pull his troops out.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iraq">Iraq</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Iraq">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/14_July_Revolution" title="14 July Revolution">14 July Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Vantage" title="Operation Vantage">Operation Vantage</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Iraqi%E2%80%93Kurdish_War" title="First Iraqi–Kurdish War">First Iraqi–Kurdish War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ramadan_Revolution" title="Ramadan Revolution">Ramadan Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ar-Rashid_revolt" title="Ar-Rashid revolt">Ar-Rashid revolt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/November_1963_Iraqi_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="November 1963 Iraqi coup d&#39;état">November 1963 Iraqi coup d'état</a></div>
<p>Historian Brandon Wolfe-Hunnicutt  has examined American policy toward Iraq between 1958 and 1963. Under Eisenhower and Kennedy, policymakers were deeply divided between a hard-line interventionist faction and a more accommodating anti-interventionist faction. By 1962, the Kennedy administration embraced regime change as the American goal. The reason was the threat to Iraqi oil installations, and not the fear of a Communist takeover.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Relations between the United States and Iraq became strained following the <a href="/wiki/14_July_Revolution" title="14 July Revolution">overthrow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Monarchy of Iraq">Iraqi monarchy</a> on July 14, 1958, which resulted in the declaration of a <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Republic_(1958-68)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Republic (1958-68)">republican government</a> led by Brigadier <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Karim_Qasim" title="Abd al-Karim Qasim">Abd al-Karim Qasim</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson20153-5_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson20153-5-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Concerned about the influence of <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Communist_Party" title="Iraqi Communist Party">Iraqi Communist Party</a> (ICP) members in Qasim's administration, and hoping to prevent "<a href="/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party" title="Ba&#39;ath Party">Ba'athist</a> or Communist exploitation of the situation," President Eisenhower had established a Special Committee on Iraq (SCI) in April 1959 to monitor events and propose various contingencies for preventing a communist takeover of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201519-20_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201519-20-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Qasim undertook numerous repressive measures against the communists throughout 1960, and this—combined with the <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> administration's belief that Iraq was not important to the broader Cold War—resulted in the disestablishment of the SCI within days of Kennedy's inauguration as President.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201527-28,_35_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201527-28,_35-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> However, subsequent events would return Iraq to the attention of American officials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201536_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201536-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 25, 1961 Qasim mobilized troops along the border between Iraq and <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, declaring the latter nation "an indivisible part of Iraq" and causing a short-lived "Kuwait Crisis." The United Kingdom—which had just granted Kuwait independence on June 19 and whose economy was heavily dependent on Kuwaiti oil supplies—responded on July 1 by dispatching 5,000 troops to the country to deter any Iraqi invasion. At the same time, Kennedy briefly dispatched a U.S. Navy task force to <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, and the U.K. (at the urging of the Kennedy administration) brought the dispute to United Nations Security Council, where the proposed resolution was vetoed by the Soviet Union. The situation was finally resolved in October, when the British troops were withdrawn and replaced by a 4,000-strong <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> force. The Kennedy administration's initially "low-key" response to the stand-off was motivated by the desire to project an image of the U.S. as "a progressive anti-colonial power trying to work productively with Arab nationalism" as well as the preference of U.S. officials to defer to the U.K. on issues related to the Persian Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201536-37_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201536-37-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following Kurdish leader <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Barzani" title="Mustafa Barzani">Mustafa Barzani</a>'s 1958 return to Iraq from exile in the Soviet Union, Qasim had promised to permit autonomous rule in <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan">the Kurdish region of northern Iraq</a>, but by 1961 Qasim had made no progress towards achieving this goal. In July 1961, following months of violence between feuding Kurdish tribes, Barzani returned to northern Iraq and began retaking territory from his Kurdish rivals. Although Qasim's government did not respond to the escalating violence, the <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_Democratic_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish Democratic Party">Kurdish Democratic Party</a> sent Qasim a list of demands in August, which included the withdrawal of Iraqi government troops from Kurdish territory and greater political freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201537_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201537-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> For the next month, U.S. officials in Iran and Iraq predicted that a war was imminent. Faced with the loss of northern Iraq after non-Barzani Kurds seized control of a key road leading to the Iranian border in early September and ambushed and massacred Iraqi troops on September 10 and September 12, Qasim finally ordered the systematic bombing of Kurdish villages on September 14, which caused Barzani to join the rebellion on September 19.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201537-38_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201537-38-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> As part of a strategy devised by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Shelepin" title="Alexander Shelepin">Alexander Shelepin</a> in July 1961 to distract the U.S. and its allies from the Soviet Union's <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Crisis_of_1961" title="Berlin Crisis of 1961">aggressive posture in Berlin</a>, the Soviet <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> revived its connections with Barzani and encouraged him to revolt, although Barzani had no intention to act as their proxy. By March 1962, Barzani's forces were in firm control of Iraqi Kurdistan, although Barzani refrained from taking major cities out of fear that the Iraqi government would launch reprisals against civilians. The U.S. refused Kurdish requests for assistance, but Qasim nevertheless castigated the Kurds as "American stooges" while absolving the Soviets of any responsibility for the unrest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201538-40_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201538-40-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1961, Qasim's government passed Public Law 80, which restricted the British- and American-owned <a href="/wiki/Iraq_Petroleum_Company" title="Iraq Petroleum Company">Iraq Petroleum Company</a> (IPC)'s <a href="/wiki/Concession_(contract)" title="Concession (contract)">concessionary holding</a> to those areas in which oil was actually being produced, effectively expropriating 99.5% of the IPC concession. U.S. officials were alarmed by the expropriation as well as the recent Soviet veto of an Egyptian-sponsored UN resolution requesting the admittance of Kuwait as a UN member state, which they believed to be connected. Senior <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Security_Council" title="United States National Security Council">National Security Council</a> (NSC) adviser <a href="/wiki/Robert_Komer" title="Robert Komer">Robert Komer</a> worried that if the IPC ceased production in response, Qasim might "grab Kuwait" (thus achieving a "stranglehold" on Middle Eastern oil production) or "throw himself into Russian arms." At the same time, Komer made note of widespread rumors that a nationalist coup against Qasim could be imminent, and had the potential to "get Iraq back on [a] more neutral keel."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201537,_40-42_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201537,_40-42-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Following Komer's advice, on December 30 Kennedy's <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Advisor_(United_States)" title="National Security Advisor (United States)">National Security Advisor</a> <a href="/wiki/McGeorge_Bundy" title="McGeorge Bundy">McGeorge Bundy</a> sent the President a cable from the U.S. Ambassador to Iraq, John Jernegan, which argued that the U.S. was "in grave danger [of] being drawn into [a] costly and politically disastrous situation over Kuwait." Bundy also requested Kennedy's permission to "press <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State</a>" to consider measures to resolve the situation with Iraq, adding that cooperation with the British was desirable "if possible, but our own interests, oil and other, are very directly involved."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201535,_41-43_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201535,_41-43-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1962, the State Department issued new guidelines on Iraq that were intended to increase American influence in the country. Around the same time, Kennedy instructed the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> (CIA)—under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Bulloch_Roosevelt_Jr." title="Archibald Bulloch Roosevelt Jr.">Archie Roosevelt, Jr.</a>—to begin making preparations for a military coup against Qasim.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201543-45_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201543-45-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> On June 2, Iraqi Foreign Minister Hashim Jawad ordered Jernegan to leave the country, stating that Iraq was also withdrawing its ambassador from Washington in retaliation for the U.S. accepting the credentials of a new Kuwaiti ambassador on June 1, which Iraq had repeatedly warned would result in a downgrading of diplomatic relations. Despite the Iraqi warnings, senior U.S. officials were stunned by the downgrade; Kennedy had not been informed of the likely consequences of accepting the Kuwaiti ambassador.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201547-48_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201547-48-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> By the end of 1962, a series of major defeats at the hands of Kurdish rebels had severely damaged both the Iraqi army's morale and Qasim's popular support. From September 1962 through February 1963, Qasim repeatedly blamed the "criminal activities" of the U.S. for the battlefield successes of the Kurds, but the State Department rejected requests from the U.S. Chargé d'Affaires in Baghdad, Roy Melbourne, to publicly respond to Qasim's allegations out of fear that doing so would jeopardize the remaining U.S. presence in Iraq. On February 5, 1963 Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Dean_Rusk" title="Dean Rusk">Dean Rusk</a> informed the U.S. embassy in Iraq that the State Department was "considering carefully whether on balance U.S. interests would be served [at] this particular juncture by abandoning [its] policy of avoiding public reaction to Qasim's charges," with the reluctance stemming from the desire to avoid compromising the CIA's "significant intelligence collecting operations": On February 7, State Department executive secretary William Brubeck informed Bundy that Iraq had become "one of the more useful spots for acquiring technical information on Soviet military and industrial equipment and on Soviet methods of operation in nonaligned areas."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201548,_51-54,_219_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201548,_51-54,_219-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The CIA had earlier penetrated a top-secret Iraqi-Soviet <a href="/wiki/Surface-to-air_missile" title="Surface-to-air missile">surface-to-air missile</a> project.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201545_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201545-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> With access to crucial intelligence hanging in the balance, U.S. officials were showing "great reluctance about aggravating Qasim."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson2015200_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson2015200-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After reaching a secret agreement with Barzani to work together against Qasim in January, the anti-imperialist and anti-communist <a href="/wiki/Arab_Socialist_Ba%27ath_Party_%E2%80%93_Iraq_Region" title="Arab Socialist Ba&#39;ath Party – Iraq Region">Iraqi Ba'ath Party</a> <a href="/wiki/Ramadan_Revolution" title="Ramadan Revolution">overthrew and executed Qasim in a violent coup</a> on February 8, 1963. While there have been persistent rumors that the CIA orchestrated the coup, declassified documents and the testimony of former CIA officers indicate there was no direct American involvement, although the CIA was actively seeking to find a suitable replacement for Qasim within the Iraqi military and the U.S. had been notified of two aborted Ba'athist coup plots in July and December 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201545,_53,_57-58_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201545,_53,_57-58-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECitino2017218-219_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECitino2017218-219-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Despite evidence that the CIA had been closely tracking the Ba'ath Party's coup planning since "at least 1961," the CIA official working with Roosevelt to instigate a military coup against Qasim, and who later became the head of the CIA's operations in Iraq and Syria, has "denied any involvement in the Ba'ath Party's actions," stating instead that the CIA's efforts against Qasim were still in the planning stages at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson2015xxi,_45,_49,_57-58,_121,_200_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson2015xxi,_45,_49,_57-58,_121,_200-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Qasim's former deputy <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Salam_Arif" title="Abdul Salam Arif">Abdul Salam Arif</a> (who was not a Ba'athist) was given the largely ceremonial title of President, while prominent Ba'athist general <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Hassan_al-Bakr" title="Ahmed Hassan al-Bakr">Ahmed Hassan al-Bakr</a> was named Prime Minister. The most powerful leader of the new government was the secretary of the Iraqi Ba'ath Party, Ali Salih al-Sa'di, who controlled the militant National Guard and organized a massacre of hundreds—if not thousands—of suspected communists and other dissidents in the days following the coup.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201559-60,_77_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201559-60,_77-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The Kennedy administration viewed the prospect of an Iraqi shift in the Cold War with cautious optimism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201560-61_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201560-61-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> However, U.S. officials were worried that a renewal of conflict with the Kurds could threaten the Iraqi government's survival. While Barzani had released 1,500 Arab prisoners of war as a gesture of good faith, Iraqi Foreign Minister Talib Shabib told Melbourne on March 3 that the government was unwilling to consider any concessions beyond cultural autonomy and was prepared to use anti-Barzani Kurds and Arab tribes in northern Iraq to co-opt the Kurds' guerrilla methods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201562-64_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201562-64-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> On May 4, Melbourne delivered a message warning Shabib of the U.S. government's "serious apprehensions at [the] trend of events" and urging Iraqi officials to make "serious counter-proposals." Nevertheless, on May 22 al-Bakr bluntly told Melbourne he "could not permit this Kurdish challenge to Iraqi sovereignty to continue [for] much longer."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201566_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201566-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The fighting resumed on June 10, when the Iraqi government—which had amassed 45,000 troops in Iraqi Kurdistan—arrested members of the Kurdish negotiating delegation and declared martial law throughout northern Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201567_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201567-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, the Soviet Union actively worked to undermine the Ba'athist government, suspending military shipments to Iraq in May, convincing its ally <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_People%27s_Republic" title="Mongolian People&#39;s Republic">Mongolia</a> to sponsor charges of genocide against Iraq at the UN General Assembly from July to September, and sponsoring a <a href="/wiki/Ar-Rashid_revolt" title="Ar-Rashid revolt">failed communist coup attempt on July 3</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201569-71,_76,_80_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201569-71,_76,_80-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The Kennedy administration responded by urging Arab allies of the U.S. to oppose the genocide charge at the UN and by approving a $55 million arms deal for Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201580_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201580-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, "Weldon C. Mathews has meticulously established that National Guard leaders who participated in human rights abuses had been trained in the United States as part of a police program run by the <a href="/wiki/International_Cooperation_Administration" title="International Cooperation Administration">International Cooperation Administration</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">Agency for International Development</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECitino2017222_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECitino2017222-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Because, in the words of State Department official James Spain, the "policy of the nationalist Arabs who dominate the Baghdad government does in fact come close to genocide"—as well as a desire to eliminate the Soviets' "Kurdish Card"—the new U.S. ambassador to Iraq, Robert Strong, informed al-Bakr of a Barzani peace proposal delivered to the U.S. consul in <a href="/wiki/Tabriz" title="Tabriz">Tabriz</a> (and offered to convey a response) on August 25. While a Barzani-initiated ceasefire would have allowed the government to claim victory, al-Bakr "expressed astonishment" over American contacts with the Kurds, asking why the message had not been delivered through the Soviets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201571-75_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201571-75-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ba'athist government <a href="/wiki/November_1963_Iraqi_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="November 1963 Iraqi coup d&#39;état">collapsed in November 1963</a> over the question of unification with Syria (where a <a href="/wiki/Arab_Socialist_Ba%27ath_Party_%E2%80%93_Syria_Region" title="Arab Socialist Ba&#39;ath Party – Syria Region">rival branch</a> of the Ba'ath Party had <a href="/wiki/1963_Syrian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1963 Syrian coup d&#39;état">seized power in March</a>) and the extremist and uncontrollable behavior of al-Sa'di's National Guard. President Arif, with the overwhelming support of the Iraqi military, purged Ba'athists from the government and ordered the National Guard to stand down; although al-Bakr had conspired with Arif to remove al-Sa'di, on January 5, 1964, Arif removed al-Bakr from his new position as <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_Iraq" title="Vice President of Iraq">Vice President</a>, fearful of allowing the Ba'ath Party to retain a foothold inside his government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201577,_85_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201577,_85-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> On November 21, 1963, the Kennedy administration determined that because Arif remained the Iraqi head of state, diplomatic relations with Iraq would continue unimpeded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson201579_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson201579-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laos">Laos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Laos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the election, Eisenhower emphasized to Kennedy that the communist threat in Southeast Asia required priority; Eisenhower considered <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> to be "the cork in the bottle" in regards to the regional threat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves199375_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves199375-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Pathet_Lao" title="Pathet Lao">Pathet Lao</a> received Soviet support, Kennedy ordered the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Seventh_Fleet" title="United States Seventh Fleet">United States Seventh Fleet</a> to move into the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a> and drew marines with helicopters into <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>. He also instructed the American military advisers in Laos to wear military uniforms instead of the civilian clothes as a symbol of American resolve. Nonetheless Kennedy believed that if both <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpowers</a> could convince their respective allies to move toward neutrality in Laos, that country might provide a pattern for settlement of future <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a> conflicts. In March 1961, Kennedy voiced a change in policy from supporting a "free" Laos to a "neutral" Laos as a solution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves199375_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves199375-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In April, 1961 the Soviet Union endorsed Kennedy's appeal for the <a href="/wiki/Cease_fire" class="mw-redirect" title="Cease fire">cease fire</a> in Laos. Eventually an agreement was signed in July 1962, proclaiming Laos neutral.
</p><p>The CIA had agents in the field and produced  a steady stream of intelligence estimates for the White House and State Department. In retrospect, the assessments and forecasts were clear, reliable, and mostly accurate. However the White House decision-makers gave more credence to alternative forecasts which derived not from on-the-scene investigations but primarily from old assumptions about Communist objectives in Laos.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkey">Turkey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Turkey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When Kennedy came to power, the <a href="/wiki/Turkey_%E2%80%93_United_States_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkey – United States relations">American–Turkish relations</a> were solidly based on the <a href="/wiki/Containment" title="Containment">containment</a> doctrine. In April 1961 Kennedy asked for a review of the <a href="/wiki/PGM-19_Jupiter" title="PGM-19 Jupiter">PGM-19 Jupiter</a> deployment in Turkey. The response, drafted in June by <a href="/wiki/George_C._McGhee" title="George C. McGhee">George McGhee</a>, indicated that cancellation of the deployment might be seen as a sign of weakness in the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>'s hard-line position at the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_summit" title="Vienna summit">Vienna summit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">Vietnam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Vietnam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In January, 1961 Kennedy assigned 28.4 million dollars to the enlargement of the South Vietnamese army and 12.7 million dollars to enhance the civil guard.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> In May, he dispatched Lyndon Johnson to meet with South Vietnam's President <a href="/wiki/Ngo_Dinh_Diem" title="Ngo Dinh Diem">Ngo Dinh Diem</a>. Johnson assured Diem of more aid to mold a fighting force that could resist the communists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarnow1991230,_268_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarnow1991230,_268-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy announced a change of policy from support to partnership with Diem to defeat of communism in <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993119_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993119-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In October of the same year Kennedy dispatched General <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_D._Taylor" title="Maxwell D. Taylor">Maxwell D. Taylor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walt_Rostow" class="mw-redirect" title="Walt Rostow">Walt Rostow</a> to South Vietnam to study the situation there. They recommended sending 8,000 troops, but Kennedy authorized only a much smaller increase in the American advisers.<sup id="cite_ref-Foreign_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foreign-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
