</p><p>During his administration, Kennedy continued policies that provided political and economic support, and military advice and support, to the South Vietnamese government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunniganNofi1999257_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunniganNofi1999257-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Late in 1961, the <a href="/wiki/Viet_Cong" title="Viet Cong">Viet Cong</a> began assuming a predominant presence, initially seizing the provincial capital of Phuoc Vinh.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993240_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993240-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> By the end of 1961 the American advisers in Vietnam numbered 3,205<sup id="cite_ref-Foreign_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foreign-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> and that number increased from 11,000 in 1962 to 16,000 by late 1963, but Kennedy was reluctant to order a full-scale deployment of troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993242_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993242-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Before his assassination, Kennedy used military advisors and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Special_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Special Forces">special forces</a> in Vietnam almost exclusively. A year and three months later on March 8, 1965, his successor, President Lyndon Johnson, committed the first combat troops to Vietnam and greatly escalated U.S. involvement, with forces reaching 184,000 that year and 536,000 in 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In late 1961, President Kennedy sent <a href="/wiki/Roger_Hilsman" title="Roger Hilsman">Roger Hilsman</a>, then director of the State Department's <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Intelligence_and_Research" title="Bureau of Intelligence and Research">Bureau of Intelligence and Research</a>, to assess the situation in Vietnam. There, Hilsman met <a href="/wiki/Sir_Robert_Thompson" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Robert Thompson">Sir Robert Thompson</a>, head of the British Advisory Mission to South Vietnam and the <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Hamlet_Program" title="Strategic Hamlet Program">Strategic Hamlet Program</a> was formed. It was approved by Kennedy and South Vietnam President <a href="/wiki/Ngo_Dinh_Diem" title="Ngo Dinh Diem">Ngo Dinh Diem</a>. It was implemented in early 1962 and involved some forced relocation, village internment, and segregation of rural South Vietnamese into new communities where the peasantry would be isolated from Communist insurgents. It was hoped that these new communities would provide security for the peasants and strengthen the tie between them and the central government. By November 1963 the program waned and officially ended in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETucker20111070_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETucker20111070-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In early 1962, Kennedy formally authorized escalated involvement when he signed the National Security Action Memorandum&#160;– "Subversive Insurgency (War of Liberation)".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993281_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993281-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Operation_Ranch_Hand" title="Operation Ranch Hand">Operation Ranch Hand</a>", a large-scale aerial defoliation effort, began on the roadsides of South Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993259_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993259-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> By the end of 1962, 109 American military personnel had been killed compared to 14 the previous year. During 1962, Viet Cong troops increased from 15,000 to 24,000. Depending on which assessment Kennedy accepted (Department of Defense or State) there had been zero or modest progress in countering the increase in communist aggression in return for an expanded U.S. involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993283_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993283-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1963, Kennedy assessed the situation in Vietnam: "We don't have a prayer of staying in Vietnam. Those people hate us. They are going to throw our asses out of there at any point. But I can't give up that territory to the communists and get the American people to re-elect me."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993484_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993484-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy faced a crisis in Vietnam by July; despite increased U.S. support, the South Vietnamese military was only marginally effective against pro-communist Viet Cong forces.
</p><p>On August 21, just as the new U.S. Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Cabot Lodge, Jr.">Henry Cabot Lodge, Jr.</a> arrived, Diem and his brother <a href="/wiki/Ngo_Dinh_Nhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Ngo Dinh Nhu">Ngo Dinh Nhu</a> ordered South Vietnam forces, funded and trained by the CIA, to quell Buddhist demonstrations. The crackdowns heightened expectations of a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d&#39;état">coup d'état</a> to remove Diem with (or perhaps by) his brother, Nhu.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993558_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993558-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Lodge was instructed to try to get Diem and Nhu to step down and leave the country. Diem would not listen to Lodge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993559_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993559-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cable_243" title="Cable 243">Cable 243</a> (DEPTEL 243), dated August 24, followed, declaring Washington would no longer tolerate Nhu's actions, and Lodge was ordered to pressure Diem to remove Nhu. If Diem refused, the Americans would explore alternative leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993562–563_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993562–563-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Lodge stated that the only workable option was to get the South Vietnamese generals to overthrow Diem and Nhu, as originally planned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993573_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993573-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, the first formal anti-Vietnam war sentiment was expressed by U.S. clergy from the Ministers' Vietnam Committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993560_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993560-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A White House meeting in September was indicative of the very different ongoing appraisals; the president was given updated assessments after <a href="/wiki/Krulak_Mendenhall_mission" class="mw-redirect" title="Krulak Mendenhall mission">personal inspections on the ground</a> by the Department of Defense (General <a href="/wiki/Victor_Krulak" class="mw-redirect" title="Victor Krulak">Victor Krulak</a>) and the State Department (<a href="/wiki/Joseph_Mendenhall" title="Joseph Mendenhall">Joseph Mendenhall</a>). Krulak said that the military fight against the communists was progressing and being won, while Mendenhall stated that the country was civilly being lost to any U.S. influence. Kennedy reacted, saying: "Did you two gentlemen visit the same country?" The president was unaware that the two men were at such odds that they had not spoken to each other on the return flight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993595_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993595-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 1963, the president appointed Defense Secretary McNamara and General Taylor to a Vietnam mission in another effort to synchronize the information and formulation of policy. The objective of the <a href="/wiki/McNamara_Taylor_mission" class="mw-redirect" title="McNamara Taylor mission">McNamara Taylor mission</a> "emphasized the importance of getting to the bottom of the differences in reporting from U.S. representatives in Vietnam."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993602_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993602-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In meetings with McNamara, Taylor, and Lodge, Diem again refused to agree to governing measures, helping to dispel McNamara's previous optimism about Diem.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993609_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993609-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Taylor and McNamara were enlightened by Vietnam's vice president, <a href="/wiki/Nguyen_Ngoc_Tho" class="mw-redirect" title="Nguyen Ngoc Tho">Nguyen Ngoc Tho</a> (choice of many to succeed Diem should a coup occur), who in detailed terms obliterated Taylor's information that the military was succeeding in the countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993610_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993610-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy insisted, the mission report contain a recommended schedule for troop withdrawals: 1,000 by year's end and complete withdrawal in 1965, something the NSC considered a strategic fantasy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993613_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993613-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The final report declared that the military was making progress, that the increasingly unpopular Diem-led government was not vulnerable to a coup, and that an assassination of Diem or Nhu was a possibility.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993612_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993612-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In late October, intelligence wires again reported that a coup against the Diem government was afoot. The source, Vietnamese General <a href="/wiki/Duong_Van_Minh" class="mw-redirect" title="Duong Van Minh">Duong Van Minh</a> (also known as "Big Minh"), wanted to know the U.S. position. Kennedy instructed Lodge to offer covert assistance to the coup, excluding assassination, and to ensure deniability by the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993617_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993617-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Later that month, as the coup became imminent, Kennedy ordered all cables to be routed through him. A policy of "control and cut out" was initiated to ensure presidential control of U.S. responses, while cutting him out of the paper trail.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993638_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993638-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On November 1, 1963, South Vietnamese generals, led by "Big Minh", <a href="/wiki/1963_South_Vietnamese_coup" title="1963 South Vietnamese coup">overthrew the Diem government</a>, arresting <a href="/wiki/Arrest_and_assassination_of_Ngo_Dinh_Diem" title="Arrest and assassination of Ngo Dinh Diem">and then killing Diem and Nhu</a>. Kennedy was shocked by the deaths. He found out afterwards that Minh had asked the CIA field office to secure safe-passage out of the country for Diem and Nhu, but was told that 24 hours were needed to procure a plane. Minh responded that he could not hold them that long.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993650_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993650-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>News of the coup led to renewed confidence initially—both in America and in South Vietnam—that the war might be won.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993651_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993651-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/McGeorge_Bundy" title="McGeorge Bundy">McGeorge Bundy</a> drafted a National Security Action Memo to present to Kennedy upon his return from Dallas. It reiterated the resolve to fight communism in Vietnam, with increasing military and economic aid and expansion of operations into Laos and Cambodia. Before leaving for Dallas, Kennedy told <a href="/wiki/Michael_Forrestal" title="Michael Forrestal">Michael Forrestal</a> that "after the first of the year&#160;... [he wanted] an in depth study of every possible option, including how to get out of there&#160;... to review this whole thing from the bottom to the top." When asked what he thought the president meant, Forrestal said, "it was devil's advocate stuff."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993660_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993660-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historians disagree on whether Vietnam would have escalated if Kennedy not been assassinated and had won re-election in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Fueling the debate were statements made by Secretary of Defense McNamara in the film "<a href="/wiki/The_Fog_of_War" title="The Fog of War">The Fog of War</a>" that Kennedy was strongly considering pulling the United States out of Vietnam after the 1964 election.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The film also contains a tape recording of Lyndon Johnson stating that Kennedy was planning to withdraw, a position in which Johnson disagreed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlightLang2005276_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlightLang2005276-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy had signed National Security Action Memorandum (NSAM) 263, dated October 11, which ordered the withdrawal of 1,000 military personnel by the end of the year, and the bulk of them out by 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-NSAM_263_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSAM_263-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek2003680_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek2003680-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Such an action would have been a policy reversal, but Kennedy was moving in a less hawkish direction since his speech about world peace at <a href="/wiki/American_University" title="American University">American University</a> on June 10, 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-AmUniv_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmUniv-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the time of Kennedy's death, no final policy decision had been made as to Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthews2011393,_394_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthews2011393,_394-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Sorensen" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodore Sorensen">Theodore Sorensen</a> wrote: "I would like to believe that Kennedy would have found a way to withdraw all American instructors and advisors [from Vietnam]. But... I do not believe he knew in his last weeks what he was going to do."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESorensen1966359_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESorensen1966359-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Sorensen added that, in his opinion, Vietnam "was the only foreign policy problem handed off by JFK to his successor in no better, and possibly worse, shape than it was when he inherited it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESorensen1966359_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESorensen1966359-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> U.S. involvement in the region escalated until his successor Lyndon Johnson directly deployed regular U.S. military forces for fighting the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarnow1991339,_343_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarnow1991339,_343-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> After Kennedy's assassination, President Johnson signed <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Action_Memorandum_273" title="National Security Action Memorandum 273">NSAM 273</a> on November 26, 1963 which reaffirmed the policy of assistance to the South Vietnamese.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_World">Third World</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Third World">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Latin America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Project" title="Cuban Project">Cuban Project</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Doctrine" title="Kennedy Doctrine">Kennedy Doctrine</a></div>

<p>The main new Kennedy initiative was the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Progress" title="Alliance for Progress">Alliance for Progress</a>. Its goals included long-term permanent improvement in living conditions. The methods included advancing industrialization, increasing their exports and diversifying the products exported, reducing trade barriers between Latin American countries, and improving their communications systems. The primary tactics were loans from the US government and cash grants. At a theoretical level, Kennedy’s planners hoped to reverse the under-development of the region and its dependency on North America.  There was a fear that if the United States neglected the region, Castro’s Cuba would introduce anti-American political and economic changes.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Kennedy administration came to power in wake of the radicalization of <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, and saw the region as a Cold War battleground. Kennedy believed communism could be thwarted by economic modernization through the Alliance for Progress. Although it achieved far less than Kennedy had hoped, its ideals, together with Kennedy's personal qualities, gave him an unusual and lasting degree of popularity in Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabe1999&#91;httpsbooksgooglenobooksidhVhuAwAAQBAJpgPA1_1–3&#93;_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabe1999[httpsbooksgooglenobooksidhVhuAwAAQBAJpgPA1_1–3]-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>  The administration presided over a number of covert interventions, and according to historian <a href="/wiki/Stephen_G._Rabe" title="Stephen G. Rabe">Stephen G. Rabe</a>, "demonstrably bolstered regimes and groups that were undemocratic, conservative, and frequently repressive."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabe1999&#91;httpsbooksgooglenobooksidhVhuAwAAQBAJpgPA1_195–199&#93;_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabe1999[httpsbooksgooglenobooksidhVhuAwAAQBAJpgPA1_195–199]-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1961, Kennedy toured Puerto Rico, Venezuela and Colombia.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy's sanguine welcome <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Richard_Nixon%27s_motorcade" title="Attack on Richard Nixon&#39;s motorcade">stood in sharp contrast</a> to then-Vice President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>'s Latin America tour of May 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabe1999&#91;httpsbooksgooglenobooksidhVhuAwAAQBAJpgPA101_101&#93;_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabe1999[httpsbooksgooglenobooksidhVhuAwAAQBAJpgPA101_101]-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> On Kennedy's departure from <a href="/wiki/Caracas" title="Caracas">Caracas</a>, President <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B3mulo_Betancourt" title="Rómulo Betancourt">Rómulo Betancourt</a> said that "we receive as friends those who are our friends."<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His 1962 trip to <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> evoked an enthusiastic response to his Alliance for Progress vision. In that year Mexican President <a href="/wiki/Adolfo_L%C3%B3pez_Mateos" title="Adolfo López Mateos">Adolfo López Mateos</a> told Kennedy that for the sake of improvement of the <a href="/wiki/Mexico_%E2%80%93_United_States_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexico – United States relations">Mexican–American relations</a> the <a href="/wiki/Chamizal_dispute" title="Chamizal dispute">Chamizal dispute</a> should be solved. The U.S. and Mexican joint efforts in that field ultimately produced the <a href="/wiki/Chamizal_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Chamizal Convention">Chamizal Convention</a>.
</p><p><br />
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Nations">New Nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: New Nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana,_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_(JFKWHP-AR6409-B).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana%2C_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_%28JFKWHP-AR6409-B%29.jpg/210px-President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana%2C_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_%28JFKWHP-AR6409-B%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana%2C_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_%28JFKWHP-AR6409-B%29.jpg/315px-President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana%2C_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_%28JFKWHP-AR6409-B%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana%2C_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_%28JFKWHP-AR6409-B%29.jpg/420px-President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana%2C_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_%28JFKWHP-AR6409-B%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="787" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_John_F._Kennedy_Meets_with_the_President_of_the_Republic_of_Ghana,_Osagyefo_Dr._Kwame_Nkrumah_(JFKWHP-AR6409-B).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy with <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah" title="Kwame Nkrumah">Kwame Nkrumah</a>, the first president of an independent <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>, March 1961.</div></div></div>
<p>Between 1960 and 1963, twenty-four <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_by_date_of_formation" title="List of sovereign states by date of formation">countries</a> gained independence as the process of <a href="/wiki/Decolonization" title="Decolonization">decolonization</a> continued. They aa joined the "<a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>." Many sought to avoid close alignment with either the United States or the Soviet Union. In 1961, the leaders of India, <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>, Indonesia, Egypt, and Ghana created the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>.  Instead of encouraging this development Kennedy wantesd them to look to the U.S. as a rile model. He wooed their leaders, expanding economic aid and appointing knowledgeable ambassadors.  He placed a special emphasis on Africa, and he forged close relationships with several African leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy considered the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">Congo Crisis</a> to be one of the most important foreign policy issues facing his presidency, and he supported a UN operation that prevented the secession of the <a href="/wiki/State_of_Katanga" title="State of Katanga">State of Katanga</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy sought closer relations with Indian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a> through increased economic and a tilt away from <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, but made little progress in bringing India closer to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring2008712–713_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring2008712–713-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy also hoped to minimize Soviet influence in Egypt through good relations with President <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, but Nasser's <a href="/wiki/Arab_Cold_War" title="Arab Cold War">hostility</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a> closed off the possibility of closer relations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring2008713–714_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring2008713–714-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In Southeast Asia, Kennedy helped mediate the <a href="/wiki/West_New_Guinea_dispute" title="West New Guinea dispute">West New Guinea dispute</a>, convincing Indonesia and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> to agree to <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Free_Choice" title="Act of Free Choice">a plebiscite</a> to determine the status of <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_New_Guinea" title="Netherlands New Guinea">Dutch New Guinea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006254–255_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006254–255-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His administration established the <a href="/wiki/Food_for_Peace" title="Food for Peace">Food for Peace</a> program and the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Corps" title="Peace Corps">Peace Corps</a> to provide aid to developing countries in various ways.  Together with the Alliance for Progress in Latin America they promoted modernization and development in poor nations.  Food for Peace program became a central element in American foreign policy. It eventually helped many countries to develop their economies and become commercial import customers.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The Peace Corps grew to 5,000 members by March 1963 and 10,000 the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002606–607_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002606–607-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JFKWHP-KN-C18365.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/JFKWHP-KN-C18365.jpg/220px-JFKWHP-KN-C18365.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/JFKWHP-KN-C18365.jpg/330px-JFKWHP-KN-C18365.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/JFKWHP-KN-C18365.jpg/440px-JFKWHP-KN-C18365.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="978" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JFKWHP-KN-C18365.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>G. Mennen Williams (right)with <a href="/wiki/President_of_Tanganyika" class="mw-redirect" title="President of Tanganyika">President of Tanganyika</a> <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a> (center) and President Kennedy in 1963</div></div></div>
<p>Kennedy had a special interest in Africa. In 1959 he chaired the new subcommittee on Africa of the Senate Foreign Relations Committee. During the election campaign, Kennedy managed to mention Africa nearly 500 times, often attacking the Eisenhower administration for losing ground on that continent: "We have neglected and ignored the needs and aspirations of the African people. The word is out – and spreading like wildfire in nearly 1000 languages and dialects – that it is no longer necessary to remain poor or forever in bondage." He named <a href="/wiki/G._Mennen_Williams" title="G. Mennen Williams">G. Mennen Williams</a> as his Assistant Secretary of State for African affairs, directing him to tell African leaders that we wanted friendship with them and we wanted to recognize their independence.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy named young appointees to several embassies, such as <a href="/wiki/William_Attwood" title="William Attwood">William Attwood</a> to <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a> and William P. Mahoney to <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>. Other appointees included scholar John Badeau (to Egypt), <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal Democrats</a> with government experience Philip Kaiser, John Ferguson and James Loeb (to <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> and Guinea). The Kennedy administration believed that the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Africa#United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Colonial Africa">British African colonies</a> would soon achieve independence.  According to Nigerian diplomat Samuel Ibe, "with Kennedy there were sparks"; the Prime Minister of <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Abboud" title="Ibrahim Abboud">Ibrahim Abboud</a>, cherishing a hunting rifle Kennedy gave him, expressed the wish to go out on <a href="/wiki/Safari" title="Safari">safari</a> with Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the spring of 1962 the new style aid made its way to <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a>. On his return from Washington to <a href="/wiki/Conakry" title="Conakry">Conakry</a>, Guinean leader <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9" title="Ahmed Sékou Touré">Ahmed Sékou Touré</a> reported to his people that he and Guinean delegation found in Kennedy "a man quite open to African problems and determined to promote the American contribution to their happy solution". Touré also expressed his satisfaction about the "firmness with which the United States struggles against <a href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination">racial discrimination</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Congo_Crisis">Congo Crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Congo Crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyrille_Adoula_and_JFK_1962_b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Cyrille_Adoula_and_JFK_1962_b.jpg/220px-Cyrille_Adoula_and_JFK_1962_b.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Cyrille_Adoula_and_JFK_1962_b.jpg/330px-Cyrille_Adoula_and_JFK_1962_b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Cyrille_Adoula_and_JFK_1962_b.jpg/440px-Cyrille_Adoula_and_JFK_1962_b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="952" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyrille_Adoula_and_JFK_1962_b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President Kennedy with Congolese Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Cyrille_Adoula" title="Cyrille Adoula">Cyrille Adoula</a> in 1962</div></div></div>
<p>Of all of the Africa-related issues confronting Kennedy upon assuming the presidency, none were handled very well.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">Congo Crisis</a> was the most pressing. According to White House aide <a href="/wiki/Roger_Hilsman" title="Roger Hilsman">Roger Hilsman</a>, "history could have hardly devised a more baffling and frustrating test" for the administration than the situation in the Congo.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo_(L%C3%A9opoldville)" title="Republic of the Congo (Léopoldville)">Republic of the Congo</a> was given its independence from Belgian colonial rule on June 30, 1960, but quickly fell into chaos five days later when the army mutinied. On July 11, the breakaway <a href="/wiki/State_of_Katanga" title="State of Katanga">State of Katanga</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mo%C3%AFse_Tshombe" title="Moïse Tshombe">Moïse Tshombe</a> declared independence from the Congo, followed the next month by <a href="/wiki/South_Kasai" title="South Kasai">South Kasai</a>. Both had the support of the Belgian government. On July 13 the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> authorized the formation of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Operation_in_the_Congo" title="United Nations Operation in the Congo">United Nations Operation in the Congo</a> (known as ONUC) to help restore order in the country. The Eisenhower administration hoped to reach a diplomatic solution before the Soviet Union intervened. Attempts to exert influence on Congolese Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Patrice_Lumumba" title="Patrice Lumumba">Patrice Lumumba</a> failed, who alternatively brought in Soviet assistance to aid in suppressing the secessionist states. Plans were drawn up by the United States government to depose Lumumba, including an assassination plot. However, on September 5 the prime minister was dismissed by Congolese President <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Kasa-Vubu" title="Joseph Kasa-Vubu">Joseph Kasa-Vubu</a>. Lumumba contested the action, and on September 14 Colonel <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Mobutu" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Mobutu">Joseph Mobutu</a> launched a coup which definitively removed him from power and ordered the Soviets to leave the country.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> On 27 November Lumumba fled the capital to form his own government in east with his deputy, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Gizenga" title="Antoine Gizenga">Antoine Gizenga</a>. With technical support from the United States and Belgium, Mobutu's troops managed to arrest him before he could succeed in reaching <a href="/wiki/Kisangani" title="Kisangani">Stanleyville</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nzongola-ntalaja110_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nzongola-ntalaja110-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> On 17 January 1961 discipline at the army base where Lumumba was detained faltered and he was flown to <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lisabethville" class="mw-redirect" title="Élisabethville">Élisabethville</a>, Katanga. Once there, he was brutally tortured at the hands of Tshombe and subsequently executed via firing squad.<sup id="cite_ref-gondola126–127_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gondola126–127-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy and his incoming advisers were apparently unaware of the CIA's involvement in Lumumba's death.<sup id="cite_ref-ashton116_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashton116-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In fact, Kennedy wasn't even aware Lumumba had been killed until 13 February.<sup id="cite_ref-douglass212_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-douglass212-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> He had been of the opinion that Lumumba, though not to resume power, was to be released from prison.
</p><p>Deputy Assistant Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/J._Wayne_Fredericks" title="J. Wayne Fredericks">J. Wayne Fredericks</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_African_Affairs" title="Bureau of African Affairs">Bureau of African Affairs</a>, the Kennedy administration's leading specialist on Africa, played a major role in constructing American policy for the suppression of Katanga.<sup id="cite_ref-gibbs113_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gibbs113-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On October 2, 1962 Kennedy signed United Nations bond issue bill to ensure American assistance in financing United Nations peacekeeping operations in the Congo and elsewhere. Around this time, the Kennedy Administration was making private attempts to convince Tshombe to reunite the breakaway Katanga that he led with the Congo, in advance of UN intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_Corps">Peace Corps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Peace Corps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg" title="File:JFK Establishment of the Peace Corps edit.ogg">Establishment of the Peace Corps</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="82.337959183673" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (71 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="71292" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/56/JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg/JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="135424" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">John F. Kennedy's announcement of the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Corps" title="Peace Corps">Peace Corps</a></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Peace_Corps" title="Peace Corps">Peace Corps</a></div>
<p>An agency to enable Americans to volunteer in developing countries appealed to Kennedy because it fit in with his campaign themes of self-sacrifice and volunteerism, while also providing a way to redefine American relations with the Third World.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Upon taking office, Kennedy issued an executive order establishing the Peace Corps, and he named his brother-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Sargent_Shriver" title="Sargent Shriver">Sargent Shriver</a>, as the agency's first director. Shriver, not Kennedy, energetically lobbied Congress for approval.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy proudly took the credit, and ensured that it remained free of CIA influence. He largely left its administration to Shriver. To avoid the appearance of favoritism to the Catholic Church, the Corps did not place its volunteers with any religious agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> In the first twenty-five years, more than 100,000 Americans served in 44 countries as part of the program. Most volunteers taught English in local schools, but many became involved in activities like construction and food delivery.  Shriver practiced affirmative action, and women comprised about 40 percent of the first 7000 volunteers. However given the paucity of black college graduates, racial minorities never reached five percent. The Corps developed its own training program, based on nine weeks at an American university, with a focus on conversational language, world affairs, and desired job skills.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> That was followed by three weeks at a Peace Corps camp in Puerto Rico, and week or two of orientation the home and the host country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBernstein1991259-79_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBernstein1991259-79-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modernization">Modernization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Modernization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Modernization_theory" title="Modernization theory">Modernization theory</a></div>
<p>Kennedy relied on economists <a href="/wiki/W.W._Rostow" class="mw-redirect" title="W.W. Rostow">W.W. Rostow</a> on his staff and outsider <a href="/wiki/John_Kenneth_Galbraith" title="John Kenneth Galbraith">John Kenneth Galbraith</a> for ideas on how to promote rapid economic development in the "<a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>". They promoted modernization models in order to reorient American aid to Asia, Africa and Latin America. In the Rostow version in his <i>The Stages of Economic Growth</i> (1960) progress must pass through five stages, and for underdeveloped world the critical stages were the second one, the transition, the third stage, the takeoff into self-sustaining growth. Rostow argued that American intervention could propel a country from the second to the third stage he expected that once it reached maturity, it would have a large  energized middle class that would establish democracy and civil liberties and institutionalize human rights. The result was a comprehensive theory that could be used to challenge Marxist ideologies, and thereby repel communist advances. <sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>   The model provided the foundation for the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Progress" title="Alliance for Progress">Alliance for Progress</a> in Latin America, the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Corps" title="Peace Corps">Peace Corps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_for_Peace" title="Food for Peace">Food for Peace</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">Agency for International Development</a> (AID), and numerous programs in South Vietnam, especially building strategic hamlets against the communist threat.  Kennedy proclaimed the 1960s the "Development Decade" and substantially increased the budget for foreign assistance. Modernization theory supplied the design, rationale, and justification for these programs. The goals proved much too ambitious, and the economists in a few years abandoned the European-based  modernization model as inappropriate to the cultures they were trying to impact. <sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy and his top advisers were working from implicit ideological assumptions regarding <a href="/wiki/Modernization" class="mw-redirect" title="Modernization">modernization</a>. They firmly believed modernity was not only good for the target populations, but was essential to avoid communism on the one hand or extreme control of traditional rural  society by the very rich landowners on the other. They believed America had a duty, as the most modern country in the world, to promulgate this ideal to the poor nations of the Third World. They wanted programs that were altruistic, and benevolent—and also tough, energetic, and determined. It was benevolence with a foreign policy purpose.  Michael Latham has identified how this ideology worked out in three major programs the Alliance for Progress, the Peace Corps, and the strategic hamlet program in South Vietnam. However, Latham argues that the  ideology was a non-coercive version of the modernization goals of the imperialistic of Britain, France and other European  countries in the 19th century .<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_policy">Trade policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Trade policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Round" title="Kennedy Round">Kennedy Round</a></div>
<p>Europe had started to integrate economically  and American policy was to encourage this, and to become more engaged with Europe. The creation of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC) in 1957 lowered tariffs inside Europe. It posed a challenge to Washington, warned Under Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/George_Ball_(diplomat)" title="George Ball (diplomat)">George Ball</a>, himself a committed Europeanist who had represented foreign steel producers as a trade lawyer. The fear was that the U.S. with its higher standard of living, higher labor costs, and its insular political tradition  would see American products losing markets in Europe.  Furthermore, there was a nagging fear that the Soviet economic growth was catching up with the United States. The solution was reducing the tariffs between the U.S. and Europe.  However powerful business groups, especially  chemicals, steel, machine tools, and electronics.  They had succeeded in 1958 in blocking Eisenhower's request for authority to negotiate reduced tariffs. Nevertheless, Kennedy pressed for the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Trade_Expansion_Act" title="Trade Expansion Act">Trade Expansion Act</a> of 1962, which gave the president authority to decrease duties up to 50% from their 1962 levels or increase them up to 50% from their 1934 levels.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>  After the act was passed, the administration pressed for a new round of multilateral trade talks to utilize its new authority, which would become known as the Kennedy Round as a memorial after Kennedy's death.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Foreign_policy_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_administration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In terms of evaluating Kennedy's foreign policy, historians and biographers have been deeply split between highly favorable and quite negative.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>  One group  praised Kennedy as a consummate pragmatist, skilled crisis manager, and, indeed, a great world leader. The full disaster in Vietnam had not yet played out when they wrote.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>  They included Arthur Schlesinger Jr., Theodore Sorensen, and Roger Hilsman.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>  The opposition, angered and animated by the Vietnam War, launched their attack in the 1970s, focusing mostly on his responsibility for escalating the Vietnam War, his imperialism regarding Latin America and Africa, and his repeated promises to be the aggressive cold warrior who would challenge the Soviets more vigorously than Eisenhower did. They included  David Halberstam, Louise Fitzsimons, Richard J. Walton, and Henry Fairlie.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Vietnam and the Cold War are the two major issues that faced the Kennedy presidency. Historians disagree. However, there is general scholarly agreement that his presidency was successful on a number of lesser issues. Thomas Paterson finds that the Kennedy administration helped quiet the crisis over Laos;  was suitably cautious about the Congo; liberalized trade; took the lead in humanitarianism especially with the Peace Corps; helped solve a nasty dispute between Indonesia and the Netherlands; achieve the Limited Test Man Treaty; created a new Arms Control and Disarmament Agency; defended Berlin; and strengthened European defenses. His willingness to negotiate with Khrushchev smoothed the Berlin crisis, and Kennedy's personal diplomacy earned him the respect of Third World leaders. <sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the two major issues, no consensus has been reached. Michael L. Krenn argues in 2017:
</p>
<dl><dd>Fifty-some years after his assassination, John F. Kennedy remains an enigma. Was he the brash and impulsive president who brought the world to the brink of World War III with the Cuban Missile Crisis? Or was he the brave challenger of the American military-industrial complex who would have prevented the Vietnam War? Various studies portray him as a Cold War liberal, or a liberal Cold Warrior, or come up with pithy phrases to summarize the man and his foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
